Childless Dog Woman.
22:30 Hypoxia / Mountain sickness
1st time I did Mount Washington I got caught in a white out and freeze.  Along  the way met up with a fairly famous mountaineer, that had just taken a wicked hard fall, as I was coming around a bend. It had been a beautiful late fall day on the way up. Then bam white out. He was scrambling down and slipped at the top of the ledge I was about to go up. Hit with a thud on his side, then popped up cursing loudly looking like Thor himself. Saw me and said "oops sorry brother" both started laughing as I asked if he was okay. He was like "uhh maybe" Truth was we were both injured, I had already taken a few good falls that only my mountain pack saved my spine on, when feet flew out forward stepping of frozen boulders, and both knees hyper extended to the side from a bunch of the same kind of slipping on iced up rocks. I asked him if I should make a dash to the last clibers hut.. he looked back up the trail, considering the idea him self out loud, looked at me and said, "it's always has to be the choice of each of us right ? , but I'm going down with equal risk at this point" gave me a hard bear paw to my shoulder a hug, and took off scrambling down the trail and disappeared into the white.  I wasn't scrambling back down and had to consider conditions and distances up or down. After a minute or two back was best..left the trail though and worked my way over to the trad ski trail, sliding down on my butt  using rain poncho like a sled, for what felt like a mile, then used the creek after the water falls , knowing it went back to pinkham notch lodge. By the time I got to the Jeep there was a foot of snow covering everything. The lodge let me know my stranger friend made it back. Got a coffee and a meal, stared quietly into the fireplace not looking around or speaking to others there, just meditating on what I just experienced. Realizing how close to being a statistic on the wall we were ...Then got up, limped out to keep barely managing stairs. Could hardly get into the jeep, body completely stiffening, stepping on the clutch made me groan, but managed to drive and stay on the mountain pass back to North Conway and my apartment on THE 3RD FLOOR! hardest climb of that day. Made a large pot of hot chocolate, and got another pot of stew warming, while the tub was filled with hot water only, and as I zoned out the window below, watching the Saco River start to rise and flow with trees floating down it. More realization I'd probably been killed if I kept going up or got incapable . Just getting boots off and undressed was slow and difficult, likely due to hypothermia . It's good that I shut off the burners to stove, cause after getting into what felt like a tub of lava, I quickly fell asleep until the water was no longer warm. But I was moving better. Ate, drank mug after mug of hot chocolate... Got in bed and only got out of it to pee for 3 days. Took weeks for my legs to heal. But I got it the following spring , trying to figure out where I was making my escape the last time.
Mountaineering vs pleasure hiking. Only run if your life depends on it, there's a danger in running on its own .

Thanks for sharing that video. You made the right decision. Pushing on to attain some goal, whatever it is, when you're solo outdoors in remote places and your body  and nature are warning you that it's going to beat the crap out of you, or maybe kill you,  is always the best  option. Then it's time to turn back, or hunker down in place if conditions aren't safe for proceeding. You're a proven adventurer but it's better to live to share your adventures and relive them in your memories.   I climbed to the top of Mount Whitney, hiked to the bottom of the Grand Canyon, explored remote parts of Baja, California, mountain skied in a whiteout blizzard, hiked the Sonora desert in summer, drove Jeeps on narrow high trails in Death Valley. I'm still here to remember those adventures because of giving up and turning back like you did.   I gave up trying to go anymore and spent the night without food or supplies in a cave between two huge  boulders on a small mountain with my college hiking buddy. Turns out we were lucky  we made that decision to give up and stay put, despite needing to be back at class Monday morning, because the "shortcut" trail we were taking in the dark led straight over a blind drop of 250 feet once you broke through the dense brush.    I once turned back at 9000 feet on Mt. San Gorgonio when my old snowshoe broke,  gave up on summiting, and after hiking down chest deep in snow and cold camping with no fire at 8000 feet where our water and boots froze, I realized that if we had made it to the top in those conditions we probably wouldn't have been able to get down off the exposed top safely.  There's no shame in admitting sometimes that you are flat out whupped and living to go adventuring another day.
"Discretion is the better part of valor."  And survival is everything.  Yours was the best decision.  Well done, Eva.

This Summer I was on the road across Europe, 7800 km. On my way back I was to take a ferry from Denmark to Norway. As I was busy ;) talking with my sister on the phone, I forgot to take a motion-sickness pill before boarding the ferry.   The sea was so so, only small waves, enough for the people to walk like drunks onboard. Some were even walking like nothing was going on at all. I asked the staff for the sea-sickness-pill, they laughed as in: ''The sea is calm, only few waves here and there, no worries!''   Of course, the car-deck was closed and I was stuck. With every passing moment I felt more and more sick. Most of the time I was sitting on the floor, holding the carpet with all my might (one cannot help it).   Finally, the ferry was docked in Norway and I had to start driving.   Getting to my car was a horrendous experience as I could not walk nor see straight, no pun intended. Anyhow, I started a car even though I was still sick as my sense of balance was completely off. My reasoning was that even though I had a cold sweats, heart was pounding like a crazy, head was 'drunk', and my face was greenish-gray, I thought I would be ok as I was on the stable ground. No problem there, right? Wrong.    To get home after this long journey of at that time around 7500 km it was just another 350 km. I set on driving disregarding the state I was in. Yup. That road was the most horrific driving experience ever. Finally, 250 km latter, I had to stop. There was no way in hell to drive a minute longer, I was done.   I realized that I was putting myself in mortal danger as I was putting others on a road in the same predicament. Had to quit and call someone to pick me up. That was the hardest part, making a phone-call 100 km to home and asking: ''please come and pick me up''.    It took three days for my sense of balance to return and about a month to get behind a wheel again. I have discovered that the motion-sickness affects last several days, not only during the time on the sea.   I am here now and writing about it. If I did not stop right there and then, who knows? If I ever get in the same predicament again, here are keys right away. No driving, not even out from the ferry. Lesson learned. And, no getting onboard a ferry without a motion-sickness-pill. Heck, no!

You are unbelievable (awesomely) sometimes.
You didn't quit, you stopped... because you hit your limit at the time and responsibly withdrew. Your discipline took over.  Quitting would be: never making an attempt again. * Perhaps if you desire to conquer the route again, just hike it over a few more days instead. You'd have to carry more weight, but you are obviously strong enough; both mind and body, for that.  (healthy) Quitting would be the result of stopping bad habits for the rest of their life, such as smoking. But one doesn't quit, the discipline is stopping... quitting is the eventual result after years of not starting the bad habit again.  I suggest to people who want to quit smoking : to stop, not quit. Succumbing and starting again is less of an emotional failure than failing to quit.  One has already proven to themselves that they can stop, the task is to prolong that discipline. I hope the context is understood.  (unhealthy) Quitting is what I did many many years ago when the cruel nature of politics within competitive sports; ice hockey (goalie) and baseball... broke my spirit. I stopped playing all sports, even just for personal fun and health.  Perhaps having coaches who didn't cultivate and nurture was a significant factor, but it's really because I never was involved for just the sake of enjoyment to begin with... more a sense of belonging than fun.  I didn't enjoy defeating others... winning is not what motivated me. Mind you, I never lost on purpose... well... maybe in darts ;) Being an ice hockey goalie, I lost way more than I won... it's probably the most difficult position in sports. The weight of responsibility can be overwhelmingly heavy... every goal is your fault, even when it's not... even when everyone knows it's not your fault, it's still your fault. I never wanted anyone else to feel like that, so I was never compelled to gloat... in other words, I was never a poor, egotistical or ungraceful winner... quite humbled actually. Now I have to allow my body to remember that I was once strong and knew my body's mechanics and to allow my mind to be focused/motivated on getting moving again, just for the sake of my personal well-being. If I hadn't been an athlete, I'd probably be dead now. I quit something I enjoyed and let emotional and physical atrophy set in, but now have a deep desire to just do it before it's too late.  I've grown restless for simple adventure and personal challenge on my own terms. I  want to walkabout and see the world, but know I wont be doing that sitting on my ass. I must get my core and being back into motion. My soul needs it more than my body really.   Perhaps I just stopped for 30 years in order to work on other things ;)  Yes, that's it, I will start again.  Thank you for the motivation.

I took basically what you took when I started), could you ditch the kettle (just use the one pot), and the tripod (use a mini tripod, and then set it up on higher rocks to get those running shots...or use a lighter camera and a trekking-pole mount)? Eva,  Being a third generation native to the county (Pierce) in which Mt Rainier is, may I suggest a couple of observations and also that you absolutely made the correct choice in turning back. In my younger days (I'm presently 69) I made several treks on sections of the Wonderland Trail. In my opinion it is one of the most scenic yet a very demanding ventures one can undertake. Still to this day, I frequently hunt Deer and Elk not far outside the park's boundary. In fact, I recognized the area which the cabin you stayed in. My nephew has one in the same area.  I would have kept the Bear Spray! Black Bear and Cougar (Mountain Lion) are fairly common not to mention Wolverine (fairly rare but seen in this area, and Bobcat. As an American and legally able to possess a firearm, in these areas (including National Parks) I carry my 44 magnum handgun which is plenty capable of brining down any threat. I would only kill any wild animal if mine or someone else's life is threatened. Bear Spray is an essential item for any back country venture. Secondly, I would suggest carrying a small amount of fishing tackle and a foraging guide book. There is plenty of natural food to sustain ones self if need be. The weather in these parts can change in a instant and being prepared for the unexpected in my opinion is paramount.  When I was a young man, I belonged to the local wildlands search and rescue organization and those that did not use common sense suffered sometimes the worse outcomes. My hat is off to you in airing this video and telling your story of SUCCESS not failure. Because you made the correct decision to turn back before you could not, those that watch this video will also gain this basic survival advice and knowledge!  As an aside, from my observation, I believe you may have been suffering from Altitude Sickness. Not sure, but your symptoms were inline with this ailment.

Even Navy Seals work as a team.  Everyone has someone to their left and to their right.  On missions they have a person on overwatch to look out for them. I'm possibly older than your grandparents and have done a lifetime of hiking, mainly in the Sierra Nevada Mountains of California. I had one of those weekends once where I was going to take a little day hike and cross over the Sierra Nevada main ridge. There was no trail, and you find your own way. On the hike I would find and truck along the infamous John Muir-Pacific Crest trail for a certain unknown distance, then cross back over the Sierras where someone would pick me up at a given point. I estimated the day hike at 25 miles, 30 miles max. I estimated the hike time to be 12-14 hours. I started at 5am with a light daypack with energy foods for maybe two meals. Twelve hours later 5pm rolled around and I realized I was hardly halfway through the hike. There was no going back, only forward. I hiked through the night with a few rest stops and now freezing temperatures. Fortunately, there was a full moon and no wind. I did have a light jacket to soften the temps somewhat. I finally made it to my destination at 5am the next morning. My pick up partner assumed that something bad had happened since I normally stick to a plan and am fairly accurate on time. The error was that over 20 miles of switchbacks along the way were unaccounted for on the trail maps. My "little" hike was over 50 miles. I was in my late 50s at the time and was still learning, sometimes the hard way. I laugh about it now in my late 70s, but it was hell at the time. I fully respect what you are doing. Keep up the good work. (I'm the guy who named a Bristlecone Pine tree after you, Eva. We'll never know, but I swear I found a tree that has a bigger trunk and therefore is probably older than the tree, Methuselah, which is the oldest known living element on planet Earth, @ 4600+ years, in the Bristlecone Pine forest of the White Mountains of California. I'm the only person in the world who knows where it is. So, Shhhhh! Don't tell anyone!)

Hi Eva, in case you still see this comment, your swollen fingers are actually indicative of hyponatremia, i.e. lack of salt. At severe levels it can be just as debilitating as dehydration, and confusingly drinking more water will make it worse.

I must say-- I don't get the running bit. And least with a heavy backpack. You were in one of the most beautiful places on earth, with excellent weather (somewhat rare on Rainier) and time to enjoy the views, the solitude, the beauty of it all.  10 to 14 days is the usual time needed to hike the Wonderland Trail. Doing it in a week would be rushed. But three days? Delusional or masochistic. I'm glad your better angels made the right call. You've got to remain fit and clear headed on such a long, arduous hike. What if a bear had found your food? What if you had crossed paths with a mountain lion or aggressive bear. You'd need a clear head. You'd need muscle response.  Finally, running with a heavy backpack up and down rocky trials increases the wear and tear on your body unnecessarily, possibly resulting in injury leading to permanent damage. For heaven's sake. Save something for the next day, week, year, decade. Your videos are very well made. I greatly admire your pluck.

i just wanted to address a couple issues you may have encountered when you mentioned you had been feeling weak despite drinking and eating. high altitude (altitude sickness) can hit those accustomed to sea level or near sea level environments much harder than those who've acclimated themselves to high altitude first. it takes a few months of living in a high altitude environment for your body to adequately increase your red blood cell count appropriately to carry enough oxygen throughout your system. to combat this you can take iron supplements. i know you are vegetarian, but beef jerky is a very good go to for the high protein and mineral content as well. by the time you get a mile above sea level, the effects of altitude sickness can sneak up on you very quickly, even when you seem to be pumping enough food, fluids, and electrolytes. the problem comes where if any one of those things gets out of balance, it can exacerbate the effects of altitude very quickly and you can suddenly find yourself incapacitated much faster than you thought possible. be careful out there. you made the right choice in stopping when you did.
Another tip is to wear a bell of some kind or something that clangs together to make noise. Bears will hear that approaching and will run away. Mostly.

I was set to attempt a 60mile hike in Indiana. I'd been training but i wholly underestimated how steep the hills and ridges were. I planned to take 5 days  and way over packed. my bag must have weighed almost 70lb. I also had my dog with me. On the first day we only hiked 5 miles ( we arrived late and the darkness set in soon after) . and the second day I walked to the 8 mile mark. It was our first real checkpoint and the water i had staged before hand was gone. I was already sore and exhausted and my dog had developed a limp. I was talking to a day hiker i met and he confirmed that the terrain ahead of where i camped was much much harder than what i had already trekked. and considering that i was out of water and my dog was injured , we had to turn around.  it took us all day to get back to the car and being there were no available camp sites available , i had to drive 4 hours home while being completely dehydrated and sick and hurting. and the biggest thing i took away from this trip was that quitting is ok if your life is on the line. A little bit on altitude sickness, since you didn't mention it.  Altitude sickness  Also called: acute mountain sickness  Physical distress from difficulty adjusting to lower oxygen pressure at high altitude.  Most cases of altitude sickness are mild, but some may be life-threatening.

OK.  During WWII - the bomber pilots would tell their crews "OK, Boys.  10,000 ft.  Time to put your oxygen masks on."  We learn from experience and making mistakes.  My _guess_ here - is that the mistake you made this time - was trying to run this.  If you had just hiked it - that would have given you more time to acclimatize to the altitude.  Thinking you were just going to blitz your way through this - caused you to not bring enough supplies if you couldn't meet your schedule.  You had nothing in reserve when things began to go wrong.  Thus - you didn't have the time to stop and rest and get used to the Altitude - and then go on.  One other thing about this - is that you didn't just go the distance you thought you did - you went through all that extra distance from having to traverse not just the distance you were going - but - walking up to place the camera, walking back to walk into the shot, then walking head - and walking back to pick up the camera.  Or ... should I say ... running ...  One other thing here is - Welcome To Your 30's.  I can remember when I got into my 30's and ... there was all this stuff I'd thought nothing of doing ... in my 20's ... that now gave me a problem.  And ... that was 40 years ago ...  You did the right thing.  You were all alone (which is actually a mistake) and you had to get yourself back to your car one way or the other.  So - evaluating your situation and making the right decision - was the correct thing to do.  Here - the next mistake you made - was getting all emotional about it.  The correct thing to do is realize you made a mistake and fix it.  _"Hunh ... I'm all fucked up ... I slept all night ... and I feel worse.  I need to adjust the plan."_  When I was in Recruit Training for the Marines - I went into it with a cold - that turned into bronchitis.  We had a certain number of sick days we could have - and not get set back in training.  If that happens they put you in a unit that is two weeks behind where you were when you got set back. No one wants to repeat an extra two weeks of Boot Camp.  So - I thought a lot about each one of those days I went to sick call.  Each time it was _"If I don't go on sick call - will I get worse?"_  If the answer was - yes - I went on sick call.  I used ALL my days of sick leave and did not get set back in training.  The next phase of training after that - was the same thing.  Now ... about those Navy Seals ...   Most of those guys are in their 20's.  Very few people can do what those guys can do.  Their training is designed to weed out the people who are anything like - normal - so that only these really exceptional people can make it.  Anyway - GL - and Stay Safe. .

You are very wise to turn back when your body was telling you that something is wrong.  90 miles in 3 days is very ambitious schedule when on flat ground in pleasant weather.  You are on the back side of Mt. Rainier.  You are probably suffering from the effects of altitude sickness, which can happen when you do a power hike up thousands of vertical feet.  Other things that could be going on is low glucose in the blood stream.  Maybe you have a pre-diabetes condition, which you would not know about.  You look like someone in her thirties, that is when the Aging Process is sneaking up on you.  At that age you spent 20 years of living in a very strong and healthy body where working out and exercise can keep you in your most capable condition.  So you push yourself on the Wonderland Trail.  Your body might have easily lost something very subtle, and now you don't feel well in a way you've never experienced. We can thank God you turned around and made it back.

That's called bonking, you need more energy gels and less hard to digest food. If you read the instructions they say one every 45 minutes and you only have 3 per day. You did great on the trip. I had an experience very similar to how you felt on this trip. I was going to hike a trail in northern Minnesota called the Superior Hiking Trail. I was going to hike from the Canadian border to my hometown of Duluth, 265 miles and take about 19 days to do it. Three weeks before the hike I injured my left knee. By the time the start day of the hike came my knee was about 80%. Because of all the planning and gear and food purchased and how fast I tend to heal, I went ahead and began, hoping the knee would heal. It's a rugged single track trail. Lots of up and down and rocks and roots. I quit the hike after five days and 52 miles. With both knees in a lot of pain.   On the fourth day I felt fine after breakfast (oatmeal üôÇ) which was my breakfast each day. I had lots of stuff in it, so it wasn't just oats. Anyway, after about an hour I started to lose energy. I felt like I had the flu as far as how low my energy was. I had a twelve mile day to do and it took all day. I made it just before dark. It felt like my body was pulling out all the stops to tell me that tomorrow I needed to stop, which I did. I actually felt pretty good the next morning, about 80% back to normal.  I still hike a lot, and did a hike in Glacier National Park this last September. Great place and a great time, except for getting charge by a Grizzly bear.   Anyway, you're doing fantastic on this journey of yours. I really enjoy the videos and you, and I'm looking forward to more of them.

At the 18 min mark it was clear you were acutely hypoxic. I think your world of experience helped you make good decisions, that people with less experience might have missed. Glad to see it end well for you. I hope others following your journey learn from you.  For those unaware, hypoxia is not strictly altitude dependent. Fatigue, hydration, hormones and even diurnal (time of day) effects all play a factor. It is rarely the primary cause of mortality, but is a strong causal factor in injury and death because we just cannot think critically, and our senses and balance get impaired. We make mistakes and pay the price. Crossing that log and rocks over the water was the perfect place for it to make an accident. I've been there.   Just saw your channel for the first time today. Sticking around for more. Thanks.

Eva  - to you and anyone else attempting anything in backcountry wilderness, pls read (and periodically reread) "Deep Survival" by Laurence Gonzales (the subtitle - Who Lives, Who Dies, and Why - is very telling). The wisdom it contains may save your life.  I am a field conservationist, certified EMR, been a safety educator, and have been venturing into the back country and under the ground (cave explorer) for nearly 50 years and this book is still a "must read" for me. You will be encouraged (and validated) to know that one of his assertions is that it is the mark of a good outdoors person to recognize when to abandon "The Plan" and turn back when faced with any of the innumerable adversities that can occur when we venture out of our comfort zone. Best of luck in your future adventures.

Hi Eva. Your story hit a chord for me and made me remember when I had a similar sort of epiphany on a trail, and a more tame one than the one you were doing. Really, RUNNING the Wonderland Traill!?!?!? I was out of words watching and kept asking myself why is she doing this to herself? I get the hike part. I get the push yourself to your physical limits part. But running the Wonderland Trail, a trail that I have always held in awe and still haven't attempted yet... walking, not running. I decided many years back to do the John Muir Trail in 3 parts and thought at the time that real dudes don't need tents. My hiking buddy was of the same mind so we set off from Yosemite Valley for the first 4 day sprint without benefit of a tent. Why carry the weight when you're going for speed at least in the first stage SB. Maybe in the higher country further south we'd see about that later. Mother Nature was amused by my arrogance and sent a puffball ice storm of frozen slop our way and froze the arrogance out. We camped too high up for any effective shelter and having somehow decided to take a "shortcut" over Vogelsang Pass put us too high to build a proper fire. So, I remember spending an absolutely miserable night in my sleeping bag backed up against a little tree offering precious little shelter. Next morning, it was a compete abandoning our plans and race down the mountain and leave the John Muir Trail barely started and to this day, unfinished. Once off the mountain, I went into the sporting goods store where I worked and bought the best North Face tent they had and used it for the next 20 years. Nature will take our attitudes and pride and smack us sideways sometimes. There is no downside in calling yourself back from the brink of disaster. Your video will make many folks think about stuff that needs to be thought about. Thanks for your huge breath of honesty on this.
Remember when you were packing, getting ready to load your pack? The two things you can never discard are your personal responsibility to your health and safety, and your personal responsibility to make the most prudent judgements regarding plan alterations.   In any and every wilderness difficulty, these two things become your own 'Hard and Fast Rules' and they supersede all other plans or schedules or desires.  These rules are UNIVERSALLY RESPECTED AND EVEN URGED. You certainly did the right thing and should be proud of yourself for having done so. As you grow in your own management of 'personal' guarding and responsibility, this experience will only strengthen your resolve to 'always' take care of 'you' first. To hell with what 'anyone' else may think. Do you know that on ships there are 'actions' that don't need the 'Captains' permission? That's right ---- doing the prudent life-saving thing NEVER needs anyone's permission and never will.  So Bravo ! to you. Hold your head high and teach others to do the same.

I am not fully through the video, but I have to say that by the end of the first day, I am observing that you meet the proverbial definition of having "bonked."  This is a situation in which you run through your normal reserves of energy, you are dehydrated, probably low on needed minerals and other nutrients, and at real risk of complete exhaustion.  I have been there before.  Perversely, your appetite can drop way off, and you need to force yourself to eat.  Usually, if you get enough water and nutrients in you, you will rebound a bit the next day.  But your body was telling you urgently to back off.  You may actually have saved your life.  Had you pressed your body further, it could have been dangerous.  Exhaustion can lead to bad decision-making, disorientation and losing the trail.  That looked like too big of a wilderness area to risk that in.

Eva is a vegan. Being in the military veterans know the real life advantage of protein during rucks from heck. Cool, totally admiring you. And I love this video and its honesty. - At 13:05, was that possibly caused by the heavy backpack and shoulder straps too tightened, blocking circulation?
I definitely think you should have quit when you did - no shame in that at all. I also think you didn‚Äôt have enough fuel (calories). I mean, that breakfast alone was less than 200 calories. The longer the distance, the higher the elevation, the extra energy exerted from carrying a pack means the more energy expended which means the more calories you need. Especially if planning to do this sustainably for 3 days. Calorie intake = calorie outtake. I have to imagine you were burning *at least* 3500 extra calories + your regular resting metabolic rate. In addition, it seemed like your calories were coming almost exclusively from carbs. Carbs supplemented with fathelp to satiate hunger in the long term. Carb storage lasts for about 2-3 hours in the body, which makes sense for a marathon - not for basically 3 semi ultras in a row while carrying a fair amount of weight with a lot of elevation gain.  Given that you‚Äôre a vegan, I imagine that your body is pretty well adapted to relying on carbs as a primary fuel source so when the carbs run out, the body crashes bc it is not adapted to burning fat reserves stored in the body. Also, fat is more calorie dense and thus doesn‚Äôt require as big of portions.  Anyways, all this is to say that for anyone attempting to do any sort of  extreme endurance challenge like this, dialing in nutrition and calorie intake is key as well as playing around with macro calorie intake is key to achieving optimal performance. Women are especially much more likely to be undernourished in these types of endeavors bc of societal pressures that they‚Äôre not even fully conscious of. Don‚Äôt train and use nutrition guidance meant for marathoners - train and eat like an endurance tri athlete. Also, I want to be clear that I‚Äôm not saying that endurance athletes should eat keto, but rather the carb loading recommended for marathoners is not adequate for a task as challenging as this and that dialing in nutrition is just as important as being physically capable of running 50+ miles.
I love your stories. I think you just took a decision which was not without risk in itself. Something was off with your fitness, and you payed attention. If you have enough fat than you should easily be good without eating is my experience. Let the bloodstream clean itself up, then your immune system works better too. If you want to drop weight.  Drop it off your main 3 not food.  Pack, Sleeping bag, Tent.  I went with Zpacks because of that.  They're expensive 7but a pack that's 55l can be 20 OZ.  Tent for 1 person for is 14 oz.  A sleeping bag for -7 C degree max is 16.6 oz.  Total for the big 3 can be a little as 3.2lbs.  For most other brands that are cheaper.  You're looking at 9 lbs minimum.  The weight difference on a long hike is amazing.  I went ultralight because I carry camera gear hiking.  I'd never go back.  There are other ultralight manufacturers.  So, look into it a bit for your own use.  But watch the weight of your gear.  You also should watch your hiking poles weight but it's not as big a concern if you use them all the time.  Although a lighter swing weight is a pretty huge savings on your body.  I have ultralights but still use my heavier carbons usually.  But my ultralights have a mount on top for a Gopro which is really nice while trecking to turn on and video and area.  Cold soaked food so you don't have to carry a burner to heat things is nice also for ultralight.  You didn't quit.  You made a real decision not to risk your life.  That's not quitting.  That's pure growth.  50mile runs are really detrimental on the body and take days to weeks to recover from the damage.  I have a lot of friends who used to be competitive in it.  Most have stopped.  A healthy happy life is what most enjoy now.  Instead of trying to destroy yourself for a goal.  I raced 100 mile bicycle races.  By the way, you're fucking amazing.

FOOD suggestion; I couldn't find a way to msg so I am using the most recent post. I noticed in all your meals I have seen no potatoes. I thought that odd for a vegan. Then I realized they are energy intensive to cook. SO here is an old Blue Collar trick for a hot lunch. Way back guys used to use engine heat from the machines they used to heat food. This was before a micro wave in every break room. Try wrapping a potato in aluminum foil and putting in the engine compartment. Exhaust manifolds get very hot. You don't want to put anything directly on them but near it or the radiator tank. If there is a flat spot on the engine itself that could work. make sure anything is sealed against fumes. It will take some experimenting but it could be a new source cooking energy. If you can get a laser thermometer you can fins the best spots and their heat ranges. next stop at a garage see if they have one.

One theme of this video could be:  becoming a "human being, rather than a human doing".   A factoid about mountain sickness, AMS, HAPE. HACE: Tolerance for altitude change has very little to do with level of physical or mental fitness. It's more about physiology, hydration, and the pace of acclimatization.  It has been demonstrated that for most, the fastest way to acclimatize is to climb a little higher than one sleeps. In some situations that's not where the desirable campsites are located...
At the ages of 66 (me) and 65, my wife and I recently hiked/climbed to the top of Guadalupe Peak, the highest mountain in Texas.   At 8,750 feet it‚Äôs puny compared to other peaks but it was still a challenge for my aging body.  My wife had no trouble with it.   You start out at over a mile high at the trailhead and climb 3,000 feet from there.  It‚Äôs about a nine mile round trip.   We had to deal with steep, loose rocks and boulders on the trail and 60-70 mph cold wind gusts the day we made the climb.   I sailed up the first mile or two of the hike with ease, which some consider to be the most challenging part of the trail.    Then something weird happened; my body instantly shut down like a switch was flipped.  I lost my strength and struggled to breathe.  Fortunately I had carried a couple of small oxygen canisters in my backpack which started me up again.   I was able to complete the rest of the trek to the summit and down but with difficulty.   I was a sprinter in high school but always struggled with distance running.  I had covid for six weeks two years ago and it did a number on my lungs evidently.

Evanston, Wyoming is 6,750 ft and so you could use it as a base for a week or so of mountain adventures to the North, West, and South while you train for a repeat of the trail run that you did in this video, if you ever attempt anything like that again.  In fact, the next time you're driving through the Western United States via I-80, you should stop at Evanston. It used to be a major railroad hub during the Old West and there are several historic monuments downtown, including one of the last remaining railroad roundhouses and an old Chinatown. The nice folks at the museum on Front Street can show you those.  Then, depending on the time of year, you can get your exercise running or biking or skiing some portion of the Bear River Greenway (skis and snowshoes can be rented at the Evanston Recreation Center). In the dead of winter I like to start at the end of the pavement and ski up the frozen river if there is enough snow on it to cover the ice. After, get a swim and a shower at the Evanston Recreation Center.  Maybe ice skate on the Ice Ponds (again, you can rent skates at the rec center). Before refrigeration, ice was cut from the Evanston Ice Ponds during the winter and shipped all over the world.  I'm told that Days Inn allows dogs if you want hotel accommodations, or you can head south down Highway 150 (stopping briefly at the roadside historic monuments near Sulphur Creek Reservoir) and camp and seek further adventure in the Uintah-Wasatch-Cache National Forest and the Ashley National Forest. That thing you pinned above about dogs in National Parks is probably not a rule coast to coast and definitely does not apply to National Forests (stop at the ranger station south of Evanston on HW 150 and ask if you don't believe me).  Another nice stop along I-80 is the Salt Flats in Utah and/or you can visit some of the Mormon pioneer historic markers along that part of the corridor like the Salt Lake Temple. Salt Lake City was the first major settlement in the area and the oldest historic structures are found there.   North of Salt Lake in Idaho all along the Snake River Valley there are the volcanic remnants of ancient Category 8 volcanic eruptions from the Yellowstone caldera. Then you can even continue on and visit Yellowstone itself. In the winter I think they have snowmobile tours through the park, or you can hike/camp during the summer. You can drive there (or to the Grand Tetons) from Evanston as well.

Siostra, I am a 1/2 marathon runner and a semi-competitive cyclist who has learned some harsh lessons in the "never quit" mantra. After a few years of repetitive injuries and battling with a lack of physical improvement, I adopted the "listen to your body" mindset. The results have been amazing from an endurance perspective. It is by no means a copout; It is rather an establishment of a metaphysical connection between your mind and body. I have been able to conquer more feats and see vast improvements in my training. However, if my body is communicating to me that it is time to quit, it allows me to take a moment and analyze the pro's and con's of continuing with the activity at hand. Dobranoc, Kerry

This is not quitting, Eva.  This is making a sensible decision for your own well-being.  Your body was making clear signs of distress.  Human body works in such a way that blood is rushed to the most needy organs.  Your muscles were greatly fatigued and started reach a point of failure.  So most of your blood was rushed to supply them with the necessary amount of nutrients and oxygen to repair the damage.  In the meantime, there was not enough blood remaining to service your digestive system.  That is why you felt like vomiting and could hardly keep your food down.  With not enough blood to supply the digestive system with the necessary fluids and enzymes and to absorb nutrients, your food was starting to create a backup.  If you would have kept going, it's not unlikely that your body could have starved itself trying to repair the damage while neglecting to replenish the supply from your digestive system even with the full stomach.  You could have likely passed out along the way.  Your decision to turn back might have saved your life. We all make goals for ourselves.  Sometimes we don't quite realize how much we can handle.  So we might pursue goals that are far beyond our reach.  A prudent person is the one who can reassess their goals and make appropriate adjustments in light of their circumstances.  Staborness, on the other hand, might at times border on folly and lead to destruction.  Through this experience, Eva, you have proven yourself to be prudent.  And that is something to be proud of!

Yeah, after living the last 40 years in Colorado, this sounds like everything I've ever heard about altitude sickness, Eva.  The nausea, inability to finish eating, the feeling upon waking of headache and being on the edge of sickness, etc.  Also, you spoke of consuming electrolytes, but too much salt while in the grip of altitude sickness can actually lead to more trouble.  You did the right thing 'quitting', i.e., wisely backing down from a challenge for which your body simply was not yet ready.  It hadn't enough time at that altitude to acclimatize (can take some people 3 days or more, with zero exercise, to do so).  Exercising at altitude without that necessary acclimatization first can lead to brain swelling and pulmonary edema, both of which can be life threatening, and might take weeks to clear, at full rest.  Well done listening to your body!  That artificial, gung ho, never say die attitude you described can, in the wrong situation, be every bit as dangerous and debilitating as allowing overly negative thinking to convince you things are worse than they really are.  Both extremes are bad, not just the one.  In all things, moderation.  Good job for seeing, and practicing that!  üëè

The longer I watched you in this video, the more anxious for you I became.....I couldn't even think to skip ahead to check if you were ok.......I could see the darkness of altitude sickness creeping up on you and I literally felt like shouting out to you to stop running, hydrate, eat, sleep and get down to a lower altitude as soon as you could safely do so! Your inability to finish your meal due to loss of appetite, nausea, brain fog and crushing fatigue were all classic symptoms of altitude sickness and I was worried sick that you might wake during the night with severe shortness of breath due to pulmonary oedema, a frequent and sudden killer in those not acclimated to altitude over a planned period of time. I've travelled with people from sea-level in Melbourne to 8250ft in Vail, Colorado in 24hrs of flying and getting no sleep and then become symptomatic with altitude sickness by the next morning. With one of our group, it was an ambulance trip down to Denver at 5000ft with an oxygen cylinder that saved her but that was the end of her ski trip. With another friend many years later, we had the luxury to be able to call a service that brought a pump-up mini hyperbaric chamber to our accommodation and our friend lay down in it and was pumped up to sea-level pressure, where all her symptoms disappeared. She was then slowly returned to the pressure of 8250ft and experienced no further symptoms. You were alone and confused by what was happening to you. Had you tried to go on, it's highly likely your condition would have gotten much worse........possibly very much worse........thank goodness your inner angel stepped in and you saved your own life.......now that is a huge VICTORY if ever there was one!! I wish you a long, happy, healthy and adventurous life!!

Another comment Eva. After watching your video I found myself going back to memories I had of Mt. Rainier. Then I figured it out. Recently I had been watching videos of icebergs calving and several of the videos picked up the sounds of the breaking ice. With the sounds of ice breaking primed in my mind and then a little input about Mt. Rainier, some old memories came bubbling up to the surface. I went back to my formative years and remembered visiting Mt. Rainier and going on some of those short hikes above the lodge. It was there that I first heard ice breaking but not icebergs, no, it was the glacier right there within sight of trails. The glacier was too far away for me to detect any movement but almost without fail I could walk along that trail and hear the glacier breaking as it ever so slowly broke as it went down the side of the mountain. A most distinctive sound. As I remembered it I felt the same joy as when I had heard the real thing so many years ago. Funny the things that get stuffed into your memory.

Seems like altitude sickness. As far as getting rescued. You would be picked up by helicopter. This coming from someone that lives 45 minutes from the mountain.
You need to know the symptoms of altitude sickness, you cannot think nor motivate yourself out of it. Any altitude above 3000m becomes a risk. If you start getting headaches or start coughing a lot then that could become fatal very quickly. It is normal to not want to eat when you get to altitude, your stomach gets bloated with air expanding (due to less pressure from the atmosphere) and you tend to fart and burp a lot. Sometimes that will lead to feeling nauseous and sick. Depending on the ascent you need to cover, running might not be clever. You will also need to go slow to help your body adjust to the altitude. So you do not need to eat, if you have sugar gels your body will keep in going for days. Your body can run on the fat that is on the outer layer of your liver for days, you just need water. Also consider packing caffeine shots, they go a long way towards boosting your body and mind. Distance running is not mountaineering, do not think the rules of the one apply to the other. Oh and do not worry too much about the negative comments, clearly those have never done anything remotely challenging in their lives. I run marathons for fun but have done a couple of interesting hikes/runs in the mountains too, and have done quite a number of totally stupid extreme events. You should look at the ascent and then decide what is sensible, looking at distance is a very poor way of planning.
The rule for tourists in Tibet was walk slowly, talk slowly, move slowly. At 40% less oxygen, those of us who ignored this rule soon succumbed to altitude sickness. Running at altitude is not wise unless you were born there. Think of the extra oxygen needed for running. Biology will dictate when your lungs and blood cannot find enough oxygen for running. That is the time to stop and recover, or to walk slowly at a pace the available oxygen can maintain.
Body says "you're done now! Time to go home!" In that theatre of pain called Mount Ranier, you better listen to your body! That doesn't make you a quitter, it makes you a survivor!

What you did wasn't quitting; it was good decision making. You had major symptoms of altitude sickness; carrying forward would have been very irresponsible. For anyone wanting to do a similar challenge, especially if you plan to travel solo in the mountains, please make sure to prepare properly! Practice with a heavy pack many times before your trip, plan a first-time route that is less remote with several exit points, get acclimated to the elevation before setting out, set-up check-ins with someone via a satellite device like In-Reach, set realistic mileage goals, bring plenty of emergency food and clothes, triple-check that all your gear is working, know how to recognize altitude sickness, and plan ahead under what possible circumstances you will turn around or exit early.

Thank you for this very important posting, when it's "life and death" as in a Navy Seal mission when others lives depend on it, then sure, quitting really isn't an option.  But even spec-ops teams know when it's right to fall back and regroup rather than compromising a mission by going into a situation that helps no one. Your decision was sound, if you'd become very ill on the route, you'd have put more lives in harms way by having to have a rescue come to you, cost resources and time that someone else who may have had an accident or problem beyond their control need for their rescues, etc, simply "to do something you want to do".  Knowing that this time, wasn't the right time to push on, is the wisest, and most "powerful" decision you could make.  You didn't actually quit, you judged the risk based on the situation and made an active decision that was correct for you.   As you pointed out in the video, if this was a trail race with support staff and emergency personnel on-course or at each camp at least, at the "ready" and planned just for this purpose, sure, the decision would be very different "push on until you can't" makes sense when someone is ready-and-waiting to help you if you need it and simply can't push on.  This wasn't that, as a Mountain Bike Patroller and Instructor, I wish more people would have the sense to listen to that inner voice saying "this is not the day for pushing more than I can" and turning back.  Just weakness alone could have turned into a wrong step, a tumble down a ravine and broken bones, and a VERY expensive and painful time waiting for and being rescued by a satellite phone call (if conscious to even make it) and airlift out.  (tens of thousands of dollars in bills potentially, plus pain and suffering of course).   You are inspiration every step of this journey, hopefully THIS step will help others know when it's okay to say "fight another day".

I was thinking of rule 8 in The Norwegian Mountain Code when watching this. The rest of them are also cleaver, so here they are:  1: Plan your trip and inform others about the route you have selected.  2:Adapt the planned routes according to ability and conditions.  3: Pay attention to the weather and the avalanche warnings.  4: Be prepared for bad weather and frost, even on short trips.  5: Bring the necessary equipment so you can help yourself and others.  6: Choose safe routes. Recognize avalanche terrain and unsafe ice.  7: Use a map and a compass. Always know where you are.  8: Don‚Äôt be ashamed to turn around.  9: Conserve your energy and seek shelter if necessary.

There is so much strength in quitting. Knowing how to feel within your body if something is just hard or if something is actually wrong is such an important and beautiful skill. I have done 2 long distance thru hikes now and I have had to quit several times during my hikes. I remember one day when I was on my last 5 km of the day and it was like 35C where I was in Croatia and I was falling asleep while walking. I called my husband and he picked me up early. I hiked the Wonderland trail decades ago. It was a great hike, but also a tough one. The trail continually goes up and down the ridges, crossing creeks and rivers that come off the mountain. There aren't many sections that are gentle or easy. I cruised around the mountain relatively easily, for the most part, though the second climb of the day was always tougher. The last two days was in the rain, which added another layer of difficulty. At the time, I was a marathon runner, so I was fully adapted for such a hike. Most people will have a more difficult time and will need to allow extra time for such a trip, or perhaps do it in stages. It's a great hike, so consider a revisit at another time. And remember, there is a big difference between distance hiking and just camping.

I am a veteran of the British army and in my time in the army the training and operational side was to keep going no matter what, and you did because others were in the same boat all pushing each other through no matter whether we were cold or wet. I now run a charity helping veterans who suffer from PTSD and when we started the charity we used to do long distance walking challenges and we have walked 13,500 miles across the UK. Now when I started doing this it was with the same attitude that the army had instilled in me of keep going but then as I started to learn about myself and those that we were helping it became more about the experience and enjoying where we were, who with and why. The best decision I ever made on one of our long distance walks was when we were walking 1400 miles from Dover in the South of England to Cape Wrath in the NW of Scotland. We had been batted by rain for much of the walk until we reach Loch Lomond near Glasgow and we were hit with torrential rain and high winds. Our sleeping bags were wet as were our tents, I had to battle myself just like you did on this video on what decision I should make and then it came to me. Why was I doing it, who was it for and why? It wasn't about me, it was about those we help trying to get them to think positively about themselves and giving them a positive experience, by carrying on it would have had the opposite effect and once I had made that decision, the only real decision that had to be made, it changed my life and how I now see things, this doesn't mean I quit easily, it means that sometimes you have to make the bigger decision, this is exactly what you did and your video will help others to make the right positive decision, well done.

I agree with the people saying this was probably the early stage of altitude sickness. You can try to stay on the same altitude and give your body time to adjust but it's always better to go down to a lower one. Since most of the trail you did was uphill going back is downhill. Running solo and having to stay in a time frame (3 days) pushing on would actually be seriously life threatening. With altitude sickness getting worse you also lose rational thinking and that's how people make wrong decisions and die. When you realize that you have bitten off more than you can chew it's all about rationality vs ego. Read some books on Game Theory. Into Thin Air is one good example, the movie or the book.   There is a point of irrevocable decision, in every aspect of life. It can be more subtle in career - but in extreme sports & survival situations, there is often a moment where you can commit or bail - but once you commit, there is a point where you've come too far to turn back and the only choice is to forge forward and hope. Making a mistake here can be fatal, and a "never quit" mentality that gets you dead isn't a *smart* strategy or the strategy of a winner. There is a point where you have to carefully assess the risks and decide if you SHOULD turn back and if you CAN. It is always better to find out that you should and you CAN, than you should but you cannot.

The reason you got sick is because you over exerted your body without giving your body a chance to acclimate to the higher altitude. Its called altitude sickness. At that altitude, the air is thinner. Your body is receiving less oxygen per breath, and then you are depriving your body of oxygen by your running, and the carbon monoxide built up in your blood stream; fatigue in your muscles, and giving you brain fog because your brain was deprived of less oxygen by all the above factors hitting your body at once. Had you walked the entire distance, you would have been fine. But YOU created the situation by overexerting your body in a short period of time.
Yeah, I used to give myself challenges like that. It built up a physique that keeps me rolling to this day, 50 years later. Unfortunately, about 6 years ago, my Will collapsed. The body carries on, but there comes a time when it's over. It's okay ... it's that time of Life. Enjoy what you have.  There's a time to push on; there's a time to rest, and there's a time to bail. I have experienced many episodes of depression when facing your kind of episode. For me, it often happens on the day before departure. Eva, whichever way you go, you will finish well. Welcome!

When I was crossing Death Valley solo in 117 degree heat (F) to beat the world record, I got to mile 45 (out of 146) and I had to stop because it was a bad day. All athletes have those from time to time for no explainable reason. So I quit. I knew that I couldn't beat the record because the first 45 miles was so slow. And because I knew there was a small chance I would finish at all. There was no sense in getting hurt or killed. And like you, wanting to continue but knowing that quitting is the best choice. It's a brutally tough decision. The one's who try to push through when they know they should stop, they get hurt or killed. You were smart to follow your instincts. I could see it in your face, that day was not your day to finish the course. BTW, I went back 3 days later and crushed the record by 5 hours. You can always go back and do it again.

when your body swells it is a sure sign of potassium deficiency. you can lose 3000mg of pot. in one hot afternoon of extreme activity. i have reached such a state deficiency that i could not get my boots off and felt boozy. each cell has what called a sodium pump and it requires a 3 to 1 ratio of potassium to sodium in order to pump out sodium. when the cells can not get out sodium they swell which is often visible in swelling under the eyes.

In my opinion. The reason I do hard things is to find out about myself. And perhaps to transform myself in good ways. I my thinking self sometimes finds it incredible that I put myself in the way of hard things. As though I walk out into the street and stand in front of a bus and get run over. That sounds crazy but,  hard things, things that are so hard we might utterly fail, do transform us. Or perhaps they just help us learn who we are. And only if we survive them. You can be an ultra-any-thing person, but to my thinking you are not truly one until you fail, and then get up and do it again. I know that is not at all the common way to look at things, but the risk of failure and the sometimes failing is for me part of being totally alive. So  congratulations. And thank you for setting an  example for people so they can try things and not die or kill themselves. Or just not publish the video.  A couple other random thoughts. After one of my hardest things, I thought back on it and somehow ended up watching myself as I rumbled through the winter forest. But I did not see me, I saw a bear. As though I had taken the bear in me and let him loose in the woods, to be just a bear. I believe that part of us is a bear, or wolf, fox, deer, elk or whatever and we are locked up in the cage of civilization and being a person. At least for me, letting that part of me be free was one of the best moments in my life.  Finally this word quitting is quite a stupid word. I find it objectionable. In this case you "quit" only to go back the whole distance on the second day. That is not quitting, that is heroism. Congratulations!

Dear Eva. I'm not a runner nor an athlete. Someone else here mentioned your symptoms looked like altitude sickness. But I recognize also something else: burn-out symptoms. You started this epic run while you had a young dog to look after who took up a lot of your mental energy. Then you could take your dog to a trusted place for a while, you were alone, and the "stress" of the dog is suddenly gone. But then is the moment your body&mind say: "I need rest". I agree with other commenters. It looks like you had altitude sickness. I once did a solo trip. My destination was denver Colorado but wanted to stop by some landmarks. I have hiked over 10,000 ft before so I was confident I could do a solor road trip. It was a road trip but I still felt the symptoms. I almost got caught in a snow storm and the wheather conditions worsen. I had to turn around and drive in increments because I felt I was passing out. I stopped and rested in my car before driving my back in AZ. It sucked. Nausea, shortness of breath, feeling like I constantly needed to go to the bathroom, I felt I was going to pass out. Pounding headache. There is a difference between quitting because you are tired and quitting because you absolutely feel something is off with your body and it's out of your reach to control it. You can always try again later. It's not a fail, its an experience and although it sucks canceling goals sometimes it's the right call.

I am glad you logically thought about the situation and came to the sensible conclusion to turn around, you can attempt the Wonderland Trail another day. Remember Vilk needs his mama. I wonder if your water filter failed and bacteria and/or pathogens were getting through into your drinking water making you sick. You may consider adding water purification tablet to each liter of water you filter. Consider skipping the gels, endurance energy powders, etc, try real food, peanut butter, nuts, oats, dried fruit, honey, chocolate bits, etc, all have nutrients in addition to kcal you need for trail running. It did not look like you brought enough food for even your planned trip, always bring a few extra days of food in case you need to extend your trip, in case you are injured, in case bad weather hampers your progress and you don't make your expected km per day. To lighten your load consider skipping the tent and just bring the waterproof rain fly for your tent and an air mattress and sleep on the ground. Leave the tea kettle at home, just bring the cup/pot for the stove. Leave your wallet home, just bring your ID. Leave the coffee home, caffein is a diuretic, it makes you urinate out more water leaving you less hydrated than you think. Stay safe

You made the smart choice turning back. Just because you didn‚Äôt make it this time though doesn‚Äôt mean you can‚Äôt make it. You don‚Äôt have to give up, you just have to prepare differently. Maybe just walking it with some of those lightweight high calorie bars is doable. You get about 2,400 calories per pound of bars and no need to carry cooking equipment. 7 pounds will keep you satiated for a whole week. That way you can take rest days if you need to or just go at a much slower pace. Definitely electrolyte powder for your water is a must. Bear mace at least for bears if not a firearm is also a must. Jogging it puts a lot more strain on your shoulders and increases the likelihood you‚Äôll sprain your ankle. You definitely don‚Äôt want to be out there alone with a sprained ankle. I‚Äôd also make sure to have a sat-phone just in case you don‚Äôt have cell service and need to make a call. Two flashlights are a must as well just in case one breaks or stops working. The power bank was a smart decision as well. A couple of signal flares and a first aid kit would be nice to have too just in case. Other than that you just need a tent, sleeping bag, and maybe a large brimmed hat so you don‚Äôt get sunburned. Oh, and definitely a few pairs of socks.

Elevation/altitude sickness? Not sure what your elevation was when you started to feel ill, but it's not uncommon for people to experience similar symptoms at higher elevations. Fatigue, nausea and vomiting are all indications of altitude sickness and you should be heading to lower elevations as quickly as possible.  Pushing on when experiencing altitude sickness can be very dangerous!. How did you feel when you got back to lower elevations? I have to say I cringed when I saw you using your Grayl as a hammer. The filter element is in the bottom of the filter and is sensitive to impacts which can cause the filter to fail and allow contaminated water to enter the drinking vessel.

Thank you for this video. Too many people tout the never give up philosophy and not enough people talk about the necessity of knowing when to turn back. Whether it's listening to your body because you're sick or in pain or listening to your gut that a situation is too dangerous, it's important not to lose sight of that. No adventure is worth dying for. Better to turn back so you can continue to have more adventures than to carry on and end up dying because the situation got worse than expected. Things always go downhill fast, and something small can turn into something devastatingly large all too quickly. Additionally, it may not be only your life that you're putting at risk. If things go south and you have to call for rescue, their lives are now potentially in danger too. I'm so glad you listened to your body and your instincts. And, I'm so glad you published this video for others to see and hear that message. As you said, it's one thing to compete in a race with support staff nearby at all times. It's something else to be in the wilderness all alone. And, don't think of it as quitting. You successfully made it home in one piece w/o rescue. It just wasn't the exact route or timeframe you'd hoped, but it was still a success. Cheers from Maine, USA :) Paula

We have a set of "rules" (they are not legal law, just rules that to go by when trekking in mountains to stay safe) here in Norway for trekking in the mountains, and one of these goes like this: "There is no shame in going back." And if you ask me, it's one of the best guidelines to go by both when trekking in nature and in life in general. Because we often to fixated on the notion of push on and never quiting, but sometimes to move forward, get to the goal and come out on the other side stronger is to quit.
